(فتح، مستخلص وكلب عقور من العقر وهو الجرح. تبيين الحقائق: ٣٨٣/٢] وعن أبي يوسف أن الأسد بمنزلة الكلب العقور، وفي ظاهر الرواية: السباع كلها صيد إلا الكلب والذئب، وقيد بالعقور اتباعاً للحديث مع أن العقور وغيره سواء أهلياً كان أو وحشياً؛ لأن غير العقور ليس بصيد، فلا يجب الجزاء بقتله، ولكن لا يحل قتل ما لا يؤذي إذا لم يكن لمه ضرر (فتح) عقور وعن أبي حنيفة: العقور وغيره والمستأنس والمتوحش منه سواء، وعنه : لا يجب أيضاً شيء بقتل السنور ولو كان بريا. (ع)
And cutting its legs and breaking its wings so that it ceases to be able to defend itself with its wings and legs. (Miskin) And milking it: meaning the value of the milk from milking it is obligatory because milk is part of its body. And breaking its egg because it is the origin of the game, so the value of the egg is obligatory, and if it is rotten, nothing is obligatory. (A) And emergence: meaning likewise the value is obligatory in the emergence (A) of (?) because of the break; because the apparent case is that it died from it. (A) Crow: it eats carrion and initiates harm, unlike the magpie and the crow of the field (?) (A). He made it unrestricted, so it includes killing in ihram or in the Haram, and what is meant is the speckled crow that eats carrion and mixes the impure with the pure in consumption. As for the magpie, compensation is obligatory on the muhrim for killing it because it is not called a crow in common usage, and it does not initiate harm. (Fath) And kite: meaning one does not kill a kite – on the weight of 'utbah, a bird that hunts the field mouse. (A, Miskin) And wolf: in the narration of al-Karkhi, preferred by the author of al-Hidayah, due to the command of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to kill the wolf, the mouse, the kite, and the crow, narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. (Fath) And snake and scorpion: because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Five... of the harmful beasts are to be killed in the state of non-ihram and in the state of ihram: the kite, the snake, the scorpion, the mouse, and the biting dog.' (Fath, Mustakhlas) And a biting dog: from 'aqr meaning wound. (Tabayyin al-Haqa'iq 2:383) And from Abu Yusuf that the lion is like the biting dog. And in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah): all predators are game except the dog and the wolf. And it is qualified by 'biting' following the hadith, even though the biting and non-biting are the same, domestic or wild; because the non-biting is not game, so no compensation is due for killing it, but it is not permissible to kill what does not harm if there is no harm to it. (Fath) Biting dog: and from Abu Hanifa: the biting and non-biting and the domesticated and wild are the same. And from him: also nothing is due for killing the cat even if it is wild. (A)
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the exceptions to the general rule of compensation for killing game in ihram. It first clarifies expressions from the earlier text about cutting legs and breaking wings. Then it discusses each exempted animal individually. The crow (ghurab) that is exempt is the speckled crow (abqa') that eats carrion and initiates harm; the magpie (aq'aq) is not exempt because it is not called a crow and does not initiate harm. The kite (huda'a) is exempt based on the hadith of five harmful animals. The wolf (dhi'b) is included based on a narration from al-Karkhi preferred by the author of al-Hidayah. The snake and scorpion are exempted by the explicit hadith. The biting dog (kalb 'aqur) is exempt; Abu Yusuf includes the lion in this category. In Zahir al-Riwayah, all predators (siba') are game except the dog and wolf. The cat (sinawr) is also exempt according to Abu Hanifa, even if wild. The Hanafi position is that these animals are not game, hence no compensation (jaza') is due for killing them in ihram. The key principle is that animals that are inherently harmful and are listed in the hadith are exempt.

Key Terms
الغراب (al-ghurab): The crow that eats carrion and initiates harm; specifically the speckled crow (abqa'), not the magpie (aq'aq).
الكلب العقور (al-kalb al-'aqur): A biting dog or any predatory animal that attacks, including the lion according to Abu Yusuf; the term 'biting' is a qualification from the hadith.
الفواسق (al-fawasiq): Harmful creatures that are outside the category of game; they may be killed in ihram without compensation.

Compensation for Predators in Ihram: Hanafi Position and Disputes
Rulings on small creatures and predators: no compensation for insects, limited compensation for predators, and no compensation for attacking predators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi compensation rule for killing a predator in ihram and distinguish it from the Shafi'i and Zufar positions.

Core Idea
Predators are game; compensation is wajib up to a sheep's value, but an attacking predator incurs no compensation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a key dispute in Hajj: what happens when a muhrim kills a predatory animal? The Hanafi school holds that predators are game (sayd) and require compensation, limited to the value of a sheep. However, if the predator attacks, killing it incurs no penalty. This rule contrasts with the Shafi'i school, which exempts all predators, and with Zufar, who demands full market value. The passage also briefly covers small insects where no compensation is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of creatures
Distinguish between insects/lice (no compensation), locusts (charity), non-attacking predators (compensation wajib up to sheep value), and attacking predators (no compensation).
Insects like mosquitoes and ants are not game; lice from one's own body require voluntary charity.
2
Examine the main dispute: compensation for predators
State the Hanafi position (wajib up to sheep value), the Shafi'i position (nothing), and Zufar (full value). Note that the Shafi'i consider predators as excepted harmful animals (fawasiq).
The Hanafi rely on the general Quranic prohibition of killing game.
3
Analyze the proofs
Hanafi dalil: Quran 5:95 general prohibition. Shafi'i dalil: predators are like the five harmful animals and linguistic use of 'dog'. Hanafi response: the verse is general; exception is limited; common usage does not call a lion a dog.
The Hanafi also argue that predators do not usually initiate harm.
4
Apply the rule to attacking predators
The passage exempts an attacking predator (sa'il) based on Umar's precedent. No compensation, even if owned. Zufar still requires compensation citing the hadith 'al-ajma' jabar'.
The key is who initiated the harm.
5
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar demands full value for an owned predator; Abu Hanifa limits compensation to sheep value for the right of Allah, with full value to the owner. The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa.
The limit applies only to the right of Allah, not to the owner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فتح، مسكين، مستخلص) ولا يجاوز عن شاة: أي قيمة شاة وعدم المجاوزة بالنسبة لما يجب حقا الله، حتى لو كان السبع مملوكا وجب عليه قيمتان إحداهما للمالك، ولا يعتبر فيها عدم المجاوزة، بل تجب بالغة ما بلغت، والأخرى: حقا لله لا تجاوز قيمة شاة، وقال زفر: تجب قيمته بالغة ما بلغت اعتباراً بمأكول اللحم، ولنا أن قيمته باعتبار اللحم، والجلد لا تزيد على قيمة الشاة وهو المعتبر في حق الضمان ولا تعتبر زيادة قيمته لأجل تفاخر الملوك؛ ولأن الصيد إنما حرم من ارتفاق وهو جناية على الإحرام فلا يزاد فيه على الدم، وقال الشافعي لا يجب شيء بقتل السبع؛ لأنه مجبول على الأذى فكان من الفواسق المستثنيات؛ ولأن اسم الكلب يتناول السباع بأسرها لغة، قال أحمد، و به ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلاَ تَقْتُلُواْ الصَّيْدَ وَأَنْتُمْ حُرُمٌ﴾ [المائدة: ٩٥] إذ هو بإطلاقه يتناول المتوحش من السباع وغيره، والقياس على الفواسق ممتنع لما فيه من إبطال العدد الثابت بالنص؛ ولأنها لا تبتدئ بالأذى غالبا، فلم تكن في معنى الفواسق، فامتنع الإلحاق، واسم الكلب لا يتناول السبع عرفاً. And mosquitoes, ants, fleas, ticks, tortoises, and for killing a louse and a locust, he gives charity as he wishes. And it does not exceed a sheep for killing a predatory animal (sab'), and if it attacks, nothing is due for killing it. That is, the value is not increased upon it, and if it is owned, its value is obligatory. Mosquitoes etc.: the mosquito, large or small, is named so because it bites the flesh. And nothing is obligatory for killing it and what is conjoined with it because they are not game; they are only insects like beetles. What is meant by ants are the black or yellow ones that harm by biting; what does not harm, it is not permissible to kill it. Ticks and fleas initiate harm. Likewise, there is nothing for hedgehogs, geckos, flies, hornets, lice, nightjars, beetles, centipedes, and weasels because they are among the creeping things of the earth and its insects and are not game, nor are they generated from the body. Likewise, the tarantula and millipede. (Fath, Ayni) And tortoise: it is from water animals, and likewise nothing for killing other insects. (T) Because it is reported: He gives charity as he wishes – this is what he mentioned for a single louse. As for two or three, a handful of wheat, and it is said a date; from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'A date is better than a locust, and if he wishes, he gives charity with a piece of bread.' And for more than three, half a sa' of wheat. This is if he takes it from his own body and kills it, because it involves removing dishevelment. But if the louse is fallen on the ground or from another's body and he kills it, nothing is due, as with the flea. Similarly, like killing, if he throws it from his body onto the ground or throws his clothes in the sun to kill the lice by the heat of the sun, then the compensation is half a sa' if the lice are many. But if he throws his clothes without intending to kill the lice by the heat of the sun, nothing is due. And the charity for killing a locust is because it is game. (Fath, Miskin, Mustakhlas) And it does not exceed a sheep: meaning the value of a sheep, and the non-exceeding is relative to what is due as a right of Allah. So even if the predatory animal is owned, two values are due: one for the owner, and the non-exceeding is not considered in that; rather it is due in full whatever it is. The other is a right of Allah and does not exceed the value of a sheep. Zufar said: its value is due in full whatever it is, considering it as edible meat. Our proof: its value depends on the meat and hide, which do not exceed the value of a sheep, and that is the considered factor in compensation; its value is not increased due to the boasting of kings. And because game is only prohibited for its utility, and it is an offense against ihram, so it is not increased beyond the blood (blood-money). Al-Shafi'i said: nothing is due for killing a predator because it is created for harm, so it is among the excepted harmful creatures. And because the name 'dog' includes all predators linguistically, as Ahmad said. Our proof: Allah's saying: {And do not kill game while you are in the state of ihram} (al-Ma'idah: 95) as it generally includes wild predators and others. And analogy to the harmful creatures is prevented because it would invalidate the number established by the text, and because they do not usually initiate harm, so they are not included in the meaning of harmful creatures, so analogy is prevented. And the name 'dog' does not include predators in common usage. (Ayni, Fath) Killing the predator that does not attack, like a lion or falcon, even if its value exceeds a sheep. (T) Where it attacks: the predator attacking him and he cannot repel it except by killing it. (T) Nothing is due for killing it. Zufar said: compensation is obligatory because its inviolability is not removed by its action, due to his saying (peace be upon him) 'The injury caused by an animal is without liability.' For this, if a camel attacks a man and he kills it, he must pay its value. Our proof: what is reported from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he killed a hyena and gave a ram as a gift, saying 'We initiated harm,' which indicated the cause for compensation.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage covers three main topics: (1) small insects and animals for which no compensation is due, (2) the limit of compensation for a predator (sab'), and (3) the case of a predator that attacks. For insects like mosquitoes, ants, fleas, ticks, tortoises, and similar creatures, no compensation (jaza') is required because they are not game (sayd) but only insects/harmful creatures. For lice and locusts, the muhrim gives charity (sadaqah) according to his means; the text provides graduated amounts for multiple lice. For predators (lions, leopards, etc.) that are not attacking, the compensation is limited to the value of a sheep (shah), even if the animal's market value is higher. The Hanafi position is based on the idea that compensation for game is an offering to Allah, and it should not exceed the minimum of a sheep; Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree. For an attacking predator, if the muhrim kills it in self-defense, no compensation is due, because the animal's inviolability is lost due to its aggression. The Hanafi proof includes the example of Umar killing a hyena and offering a ram, indicating that compensation is only due when the muhrim initiates harm.

Key Terms
السبع (al-sab'): A predatory animal that kills others, e.g., lion, leopard, wolf (except dog); includes those with fangs that hunt.
الصال (al-sa'il): A predator that attacks a person; killing it in self-defense incurs no compensation.
القملة (al-qamlah): A louse; killing one requires sadaqah (charity) at the discretion of the muhrim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the text, what is the maximum compensation a muhrim must pay for killing a non-attacking lion?
A) The full market value of the lion
B) The value of a sheep
C) Nothing
D) A camel

Key Takeaway
Killing a non-attacking predator in ihram requires compensation up to the value of a sheep; an attacking predator incurs no compensation.

Penalty for Killing Small Creatures During Ihram
Specifies penalties for killing small pests during ihram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing lice, locusts, and similar small creatures while in ihram and identify when charity is given.

Core Idea
Small harmful insects and vermin require charity, not a fixed penalty, unless they are game.

This lesson examines the rulings for killing small creatures such as lice, locusts, mosquitoes, and ants while in ihram. Unlike game animals, these insects are not considered 'sayd', so no fixed penalty is required, but charity is recommended. The commentary provides details on amounts for lice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Creatures
List the creatures mentioned: mosquito, ant, flea, tick, turtle, louse, locust. Determine which are considered harmful vermin vs. game.
Locust is considered game, so charity is given for it.
2
Determine the Ruling
For each, the muhrim gives charity as he wishes; no fixed amount. The text says 'تصدق بما شاء' (gives charity as he wishes).
This indicates a non-binding recommendation (mandub), not wajib.
3
Analyze the Louse Case
The commentary specifies: for one louse taken from one's body, give charity as wished. For two or three lice, a handful of wheat; for more than three, half a sa' of wheat. No charity if the louse is on the ground or another's body.
The ruling changes based on location and intention.
4
Apply to Locust
Locust is considered game, so the muhrim gives charity for killing it. The amount is not fixed, but charity is given.
This is based on the Companion 'Umar's statement: 'A date is better than a locust.'

(فتح، عيني)
Book of Hajj. Penalty for killing game and wounding it. For a mosquito, an ant, a flea, a tick, a turtle, for killing a louse and a locust, he gives charity as he wishes. And he does not exceed a sheep for killing a predatory animal, and if it attacks, there is nothing for killing it. Meaning: the value is not increased upon him, even if it is owned, its value is obligatory. 'And a mosquito etc.': the mosquito, large or small, is so named because it bites the flesh. No penalty is required for killing it and what is conjoined with it because they are not game, but rather they are insects like beetles... And the turtle is from water animals; likewise nothing for killing other insects. 'Give charity as he wishes' – this which he mentioned is for one louse. As for two or three, a handful of wheat, it is said a date, based on 'Umar: 'A date is better than a locust, and if he wishes he gives charity with a piece of bread.' And for more than three, half a sa' of wheat... And the penalty does not exceed a sheep: i.e., the value of a sheep, and the non-exceeding is regarding what is due as a right of Allah. Even if the predatory animal is owned, he owes two values: one to the owner, and the other as a right of Allah not exceeding a sheep's value. Al-Shafi'i said: Nothing is required for killing a predatory animal because it is created for harm and is among the excepted vermin. Our proof: the verse 'Do not kill game while you are in a state of ihram' (5:95) which generally includes wild predatory animals and others. And if it attacks: the predatory animal upon him and he cannot repel it except by killing it. Nothing is required for killing it. Zafar said: Compensation is required because its inviolability is not removed by its action... Our proof: what is narrated from 'Umar that he killed a hyena and presented a ram, saying 'We began the harm' indicating the cause of liability. 'Except the compelled one' – i.e., the muhrim in a state of hunger, for if he kills it, he must pay a penalty. And the difference between the two cases is that permission at harm is absolute, while at duress it is restricted to expiation by the text...
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Hajj regarding the penalty (jaza') for killing game and other creatures while in ihram. It begins by stating that for killing small vermin like mosquitoes, ants, fleas, ticks, turtles, lice, and locusts, the muhrim must give charity (sadaqah) as he wishes, with no fixed amount. The commentary clarifies that these are not considered game (sayd) but are harmful insects (hasharat) or water animals, thus no fixed penalty; charity is recommended. For a louse taken from one's own body, giving charity is required, while for a louse on the ground or on another's body, no charity is needed. Details are given for multiple lice: for two or three, a handful of wheat; for more than three, half a sa' of wheat. For a locust, charity is given because it is considered game. Then the text addresses killing a predatory animal (sabu') such as a lion or hawk: the penalty is limited to the value of a sheep, not exceeding it, even if the animal's value is higher. This is because the value is calculated for the right of Allah; if the animal is owned, the owner must be compensated separately without limit. The commentary engages with a dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i held that no penalty is required for killing a predatory animal because it is intrinsically harmful, like the excepted vermin (fawasiq) in hadith. The Hanafi response uses the Quranic verse 'Do not kill game' (5:95) which includes all wild animals, and argues that predatory animals do not usually initiate harm, so they are not analogous to vermin. The text then discusses the exception: if the predatory animal attacks the muhrim and killing is the only defense, no penalty is owed. Zafar disagreed, analogizing to a camel that attacks, but the Hanafi position is supported by the action of 'Umar, who killed a hyena that attacked him and gave a sacrificial ram, indicating that since the animal initiated harm, no penalty is due. Finally, the passage distinguishes a muhrim in dire hunger (muhrim mudtarr): if he kills game out of necessity, he must still pay a penalty, because the permission in necessity is conditional on expiation, as derived from the verse on shaving the head (2:196). The discussion includes comparisons with eating carrion and finding game versus property of a Muslim, emphasizing that harming rights of a human takes precedence over a right of Allah.

Key Terms
الصيد (al-sayd): Game animals that are permissible to hunt outside ihram; includes wild animals and birds, but not domestic animals or harmful insects.
الحشرات (al-hasharat): Insects and small vermin that are not considered game, such as ants, fleas, beetles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim kills a single louse from his own body. According to the Hanafi school, what is the required action?
A) He must give a specific amount of wheat: half a sa'.
B) He is recommended to give charity as he wishes.
C) He must sacrifice a sheep.
D) No action is required.

Key Takeaway
Killing small harmful creatures in ihram requires no fixed penalty but recommended charity, with specific amounts only for lice from one's own body.

Penalty for Killing a Predatory Animal (Sabu') in Ihram and the Shafi'i Dispute
Establishes the penalty for killing predatory animals and addresses disputes with Shafi'i and Zafar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a predatory animal while in ihram, explain the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, and apply the exception for self-defense.

Core Idea
Killing a predatory animal in ihram requires a penalty up to a sheep's value, unless it attacks.

This lesson delves into the ruling on killing a predatory animal (sabu') such as a lion or hawk during ihram. The Hanafi school requires a penalty not exceeding the value of a sheep, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds no penalty. The lesson also covers the case of self-defense and the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zafar.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
For a muhrim who kills a predatory animal not attacking, the penalty is up to the value of a sheep. If the animal is owned, the owner's compensation is additional.
The value is calculated for the right of Allah, not exceeding a sheep.
2
Identify the Dalil
The proof is the Quranic verse (5:95) 'Do not kill game' which includes all wild animals. Predatory animals are not excepted in the primary texts.
The verse is general and includes predatory animals.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i Objection
Al-Shafi'i argues that predatory animals are like the excepted vermin (fawasiq) because they cause harm, and the term 'kalb' (dog) includes them linguistically.
The Hanafi response is that the hadith about fawasiq does not list predatory animals, and 'kalb' does not cover all predators in common usage.
4
Examine the Self-Defense Exception
If the predatory animal attacks, the muhrim may kill it without penalty. This is based on 'Umar's action with a hyena and the principle that the animal initiated harm.
Zafar disagrees, but the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Compare with Zafar's View
Zafar holds that the full value of the animal must be paid, even if it attacks, by analogy to a camel that attacks.
The Hanafi distinction: the permission to kill a attacking predatory animal is absolute, while the camel case is different because the owner's permission is not granted.

Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Hajj regarding the penalty (jaza') for killing game and other creatures while in ihram. It begins by stating that for killing small vermin like mosquitoes, ants, fleas, ticks, turtles, lice, and locusts, the muhrim must give charity (sadaqah) as he wishes, with no fixed amount. The commentary clarifies that these are not considered game (sayd) but are harmful insects (hasharat) or water animals, thus no fixed penalty; charity is recommended. For a louse taken from one's own body, giving charity is required, while for a louse on the ground or on another's body, no charity is needed. Details are given for multiple lice: for two or three, a handful of wheat; for more than three, half a sa' of wheat. For a locust, charity is given because it is considered game. Then the text addresses killing a predatory animal (sabu') such as a lion or hawk: the penalty is limited to the value of a sheep, not exceeding it, even if the animal's value is higher. This is because the value is calculated for the right of Allah; if the animal is owned, the owner must be compensated separately without limit. The commentary engages with a dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i held that no penalty is required for killing a predatory animal because it is intrinsically harmful, like the excepted vermin (fawasiq) in hadith. The Hanafi response uses the Quranic verse 'Do not kill game' (5:95) which includes all wild animals, and argues that predatory animals do not usually initiate harm, so they are not analogous to vermin. The text then discusses the exception: if the predatory animal attacks the muhrim and killing is the only defense, no penalty is owed. Zafar disagreed, analogizing to a camel that attacks, but the Hanafi position is supported by the action of 'Umar, who killed a hyena that attacked him and gave a sacrificial ram, indicating that since the animal initiated harm, no penalty is due. Finally, the passage distinguishes a muhrim in dire hunger (muhrim mudtarr): if he kills game out of necessity, he must still pay a penalty, because the permission in necessity is conditional on expiation, as derived from the verse on shaving the head (2:196). The discussion includes comparisons with eating carrion and finding game versus property of a Muslim, emphasizing that harming rights of a human takes precedence over a right of Allah.

Key Terms
السبع (al-sabu'): A predatory animal that attacks with claws or fangs, such as a lion, tiger, wolf, hawk.
الفواسق (al-fawasiq): The five or six types of vermin that can be killed even in ihram: rat, scorpion, kite, crow, mad dog (some include snake).
الصائل (al-sa'il): An animal that attacks or charges. If a predatory animal is sa'il, killing it incurs no penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He must pay the full value of the tiger.
C) No penalty is required.
D) He should give charity as he wishes.

Key Takeaway
For predatory animals in ihram, penalty is up to a sheep's value except in self-defense; the Hanafi view differs from Shafi'i and from Zafar.

intermediate

Rulings on Muhrim Slaughtering Animals and Penalty for Domesticated Game
Permits muhrim to slaughter domestic animals; requires penalty for domesticated game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between domestic animals and domesticated game for a muhrim, state the penalty for the sarwala pigeon and domesticated gazelle, and explain the Hanafi proof against the Maliki view.

Core Idea
A muhrim may slaughter domestic animals freely, but must pay a penalty for domesticated game that retains its original nature.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies two separate rulings for a muhrim: the permissibility of slaughtering domestic animals, and the obligation of a penalty for certain domesticated game. While domestic animals like sheep and chickens are clearly permissible, two animals—the sarwala pigeon and the domesticated gazelle—trigger a penalty because they remain game in essence. The Hanafi school defends this with the principle of original creation (asl al-khilqah), responding to Imam Malik's counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main rulings in the passage
The passage first states that a muhrim may slaughter domestic animals (sheep, cow, camel, chicken, domestic duck) by consensus. Then it states that the muhrim owes a penalty for slaughtering a sarwala pigeon and a domesticated gazelle.
The passage also mentions that leaving slaughter is better, but not required.
2
Understand the basis for permissibility of domestic animals
The permissibility is based on consensus (ijma) and the fact that these animals are not considered game (sayd). The muhrim is prohibited from hunting game, but domestic animals do not fall under that category.
The commentary adds that if a domestic animal can fly (like a duck that flies), it remains domestic.
3
Identify the two animals that require a penalty
The sarwala pigeon (hamam musarwal) is a pigeon with abundant feathers on its legs, making it slow to rise. The domesticated gazelle (zabi musta'nis) is a gazelle that has become tame. Both require a penalty (jaza') when killed by a muhrim.
The penalty is wajib (obligatory) for these animals.
4
Analyze the dispute with Imam Malik on the sarwala pigeon
Imam Malik holds that the sarwala pigeon is like a domestic duck because it is tame and does not fly away, so no penalty is required. The Hanafi school argues that it is game by original creation (asl al-khilqah); its inability to fly due to weight does not change its nature.
The Hanafi proof uses the principle that domestication is accidental and does not nullify the original ruling.
5
Apply the Hanafi proof to the domesticated gazelle
Similarly, the domesticated gazelle is game by origin. Domestication is a temporary state (arid) that does not remove its original classification. The passage analogizes to a camel that becomes wild: it still takes the ruling of game for slaughter, but a muhrim may not wound it.
The qualification 'domesticated' is specific; for other animals, the penalty is agreed upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمحرم ذبح شاة وبقرة وبعير ودَجَاجَةٍ وبط أهلي، وعليه الجزاء بذبح حمام مسرول وظبي مستأنس،
أي يجوز للمحرم ذبح شاة وغيرها إلى البط؛ للإجماع، ولأنه ممنوع عن الصيد، وهذه الحيوانات ليست بصيود، ولو نزا ظبي على شاة، فالولد كأمه والحاصل أن الذبح مباح للمحرم، وتركه أولى فعله (فتح) أهلي يوجد في المساكن والحياض، أما الذي يطير فيجب الجزاء بقتله. (ط) مطلقا مؤذيا أو لا، وإن حرم قتل غير المؤذي ككلب أهلي لا يؤذي. (ط) وعليه الجزاء إلخ: أي يجب الجزاء على المحرم بذبح حمام مسرول - بفتح الواو - هو الذي كثر ريشه على رجليه فصار بطيء النهوض، وإنما به؛ لأنه يشبه السراويل، وقال مالك: لا يجب فيه شيء؛ لأنه ألوف مستأنس، ولا يمتنع بجناحه فصار كالبط ولنا أنه صيد بأصل الخلقة، وإنما لا يطير لثقله وبطوء نهوضه، وذلك لا يخرجه عن أن يكون صيدا، واشتراط ذكاة الاختيار لا يدل على أنه ليس بصيد، وقوله: "ظبي مستأنس" أي عليه الجزاء بذبحه؛ لأن الاستيناس عارض فلا يبطل به الحكم الأصلي، كالبعير إذا ندّ يأخذ حكم الصيد في حل الذكاة لا غير حتى لا يحرم عقره على المحرم والظبي صيد في الأصل؛ ولهذا يجب الجزاء فيهما؛ لأنهما صيدان بأصل الخلقة. (فتح عيني) مستأنس: قيد بهما؛ لأن في غيرهما يجب الجزاء بالاتفاق. (ط)
And for the muhrim, slaughtering a sheep, a cow, a camel, a chicken, and domestic duck is permissible, and he owes a penalty for slaughtering a pigeon with feathered legs (sarwala) and a domesticated gazelle. Meaning: it is permissible for the muhrim to slaughter a sheep and others up to duck, by consensus, and because he is prohibited from game, and these animals are not game. And if a gazelle mounts a sheep, the offspring is like its mother. The conclusion is that slaughter is permissible for the muhrim, but leaving it is better. 'Domestic' means found in dwellings and tanks; as for that which flies, a penalty is required for killing it. Absolutely, whether harmful or not, and killing a non-harmful animal like a non-harmful domestic dog is unlawful. 'And he owes the penalty etc.': i.e., the muhrim owes a penalty for slaughtering a sarwala pigeon – with fatha on the waw – which is one whose feathers on its legs have become abundant, making it slow to rise; it is so called because it resembles trousers. Imam Malik said: Nothing is due for it because it is domestic and tame, and does not resist with its wings, so it is like a duck. Our proof: it is game by original creation, and its inability to fly due to heaviness and slowness does not remove it from being game. The condition of voluntary slaughter does not indicate it is not game. And the statement: 'domesticated gazelle' means he owes a penalty for slaughtering it because domestication is accidental and does not nullify the original ruling, like a camel that becomes wild takes the ruling of game with regard to lawful slaughter only, so that its wounding is not prohibited for the muhrim. The gazelle is game in origin; therefore penalty is due for both because they are game by original creation. 'Domesticated' restricts them because for others the penalty is due by agreement.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage continues the rulings for a muhrim regarding slaughtering animals. It first states that a muhrim may lawfully slaughter domestic animals such as sheep, cow, camel, chicken, and domestic duck. The reason is that these are not considered game (sayd), and there is consensus (ijma) on their permissibility. The commentary recommends leaving slaughter as better (afdal). It also notes that if a domestic animal can fly (like a duck that flies), it still retains its domestic ruling; but if it becomes wild and flies, then killing it would incur a penalty. However, the text specifies that for a pigeon that has excessive leg feathers (hamam musarwal) and a domesticated gazelle (zabi musta'nis), killing them requires a penalty. This is because they are game by nature, even if domestication has changed their behavior. The commentary reports that Imam Malik held that the sarwala pigeon is like a duck and thus no penalty, but the Hanafi position is that its inability to fly is due to weight, not a change in essence, so it remains game. Similarly, a domesticated gazelle is still a gazelle in essence, so the penalty is due. The analogy is made with a camel that becomes wild: it does not change its legal status regarding slaughter, but a muhrim may still not hunt it. The passage concludes that the qualification 'domesticated' is specific to these two animals; for others, the penalty is agreed upon.

Key Terms
حمام مسرول (hamam musarwal): A pigeon with abundant feathers on its legs, causing slow flight; resemblance to trousers (sarwal).
ظبي مستأنس (zabi musta'nis): A domesticated gazelle; a gazelle that has become tame through human interaction.
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The penalty or expiation required for killing game while in ihram, typically slaughtering an animal of equivalent value or feeding the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim slaughters a pigeon that has feathers hanging over its legs like trousers. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) No penalty, as it is a domestic bird like a chicken.
B) He must give charity of his choice.
C) He owes a penalty (jaza'), such as slaughtering an equivalent animal.
D) It is permissible without any penalty.

Key Takeaway
A muhrim may slaughter domestic animals without penalty, but must pay an expiation for killing domesticated game that retains its original nature as game.

Penalty for Killing Game in Ihram: Attack vs. Hunger
Distinguishing penalty for killing game when attacked vs. forced by hunger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a muhrim killing game when attacked vs. when forced by hunger, and derive the ruling using the Quranic analogy.

Core Idea
Killing attacking game in ihram is penalty-free; killing game in hunger incurs penalty — permission differs.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hajj fiqh: when a muhrim kills game, the ruling depends on whether the animal attacked or the muhrim was forced by hunger. The Hanafi school derives these rulings from the Quran and the principle of permission (idhn). We will explore the proof and the practical hierarchy of choices for a forced muhrim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between when the game animal initiates aggression (mu'dhi) and when the muhrim is forced by extreme hunger (mudtarr).
The key factor is who initiates the action: the animal or the human.
2
State the ruling for each
For an attacking animal, no penalty (jaza') is due. For a forced muhrim, penalty is wajib. Note the exception: a camel that attacks requires permission from its owner.
The ruling is based on whether the Lawgiver has given permission to kill.
3
Locate the proof
The Quranic verse 2:196 imposes fidyah for shaving due to illness. By analogy (dalalah), the forced muhrim is annexed to the ill person, thus penalty is wajib.
The verse is qat'i in transmission but zanni in indication for this case.
4
Apply the hierarchy for forced muhrim
If forced, the muhrim must choose: eat carrion over killing game (one prohibition vs two); eat game from a muhrim's slaughter over carrion (incidental vs intrinsic prohibition); eat game over another Muslim's property (right of Allah vs right of man).
The hierarchy shows which sin is lighter when necessity arises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزاء قتل الصيد وجرحه
أي المحرم
كتاب الحج بخلاف المضطر، وللمحرم ذبح شاة وبقرة وبعير ودَجَاجَةٍ وبط أهلي، وعليه الجزاء بذبح حمام مسرول وظبي مستأنس،
وهي كون البداية عن نفسه وفيها بيان أن البداية لو كانت من السبع لا يجب شيء؛ ولأنه إذا ابتدأ بالأذى يلحق بالفواسق، وصار مأذونا له في قتله، ومع الإذن من الشارع لا يجب الضمان، بخلاف الجمل الصائل؛ لأن الإذن بقتله . من مالكه غير موجود، وهو الصيد. (فتح، مستخلص) بخلاف المضطر : [ أي المحرم في حال المخمصة فإنه لو قتله يجب الجزاء. (ط)] والفرق بين المسألتين أن الإذن عند الأذى مطلق، وعند الاضطرار مقيد بالكفارة بالنص أعني قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضًا أَوْ بِهِ أَدَيِّ مِنْ رَأْسِهِ فَفِدْيَةٌ مِنْ صِيَامٍ ) (البقرة : ١٩٦)، فإنه وإن ورد في الحالق المعذور إلا أن المضطر ألحق به دلالة، وإن اضطر المحرم إلى أكل الميتة وقتل الصيد أكل الميتة ولا يقتله؛ لأن في قتل الصيد ارتكاب محظورين: الأكل والقتل، وفي أكل الميتة ارتكاب محظور واحد فكان أخف، وإن وجد صيداً ذبحه محرم يأكل الصيد ويدع الميتة؛ لأن الحرمة في الصيد عرضت بسبب الإحرام وحرمة الميتة ذاتية، ولو وجد صيداً حياً ومال مسلم، يأكل الصيد لا مال المسلم؛ لأن الصيد حرام حقا الله تعالى والمال حرام حقاً للعبد، فكان الترجيح لحق العبد لافتقاره، وحاصل المسألة: أن المضطر إذا قتل صيداً عند المخمصة ،وأكله يجب عليه الجزاء مع الإذن من الشارع؛ لأن الكلام في الفعل الاختياري من الحيوان كما في الصورة الأولى، لا بآفة سماوية كما في المضطر. (فتح، عيني)
Penalty for killing game and wounding it – i.e., the muhrim – in the book of Hajj, unlike the one forced (by hunger). And for the muhrim it is permissible to slaughter a sheep, cow, camel, chicken, and domestic duck. And penalty is due upon slaughtering a pigeon with feathered legs (hamam masrul) and a tame gazelle (dhabi musta'nis). And it is that the initiation (of attack) is from the animal itself. In it is clarification that if the initiation was from the predatory animal, nothing is obligatory. Because when it initiates harm, it joins the category of fasiq (noxious animals), and one is permitted to kill it. And with permission from the Lawgiver, no guarantee is obligatory, unlike the case of a camel that attacks, because permission to kill it from its owner does not exist, and it is game. (Fath, Mustakhlas) Unlike the one forced (by hunger): i.e., the muhrim in a state of extreme hunger, if he kills it, penalty is due. (T) The difference between the two cases is that permission upon harm is absolute, while upon necessity is restricted by kaffarah according to the text, namely His statement: "So whoever among you is ill or has an ailment from his head, then a ransom of fasting..." (al-Baqarah 2:196). Although it is revealed concerning the excused shaver, the one forced (by hunger) is annexed to it by indication. And if the muhrim is forced to eat carrion and kill game, he eats the carrion and does not kill it, because in killing game there is committing two prohibitions: eating and killing, while in eating carrion there is committing one prohibition, so it is lighter. And if he finds game slaughtered by a muhrim, he eats the game and leaves the carrion, because the prohibition in game is incidental due to ihram, while the prohibition of carrion is intrinsic. And if he finds live game and a Muslim's property, he eats the game not the Muslim's property, because game is forbidden as a right of Allah, while property is forbidden as a right of the slave, so preference is given to the slave's right due to his need. The summary of the issue: that the one forced (by hunger) if he kills game during extreme hunger and eats it, penalty is due upon him despite permission from the Lawgiver, because the discussion is about the voluntary action of the animal as in the first scenario, not a heavenly affliction as in the forced case. (Fath, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage discusses the penalty for killing game while in ihram, distinguishing two scenarios. First, if the animal initiates aggression (like a predatory animal), it is considered noxious (fasiq) and the muhrim is permitted to kill it without any penalty, because the Lawgiver has given implicit permission. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas. An exception is a camel that attacks, because permission from its owner is lacking. Second, if the muhrim is forced by extreme hunger (mudtarr) and kills game, penalty is obligatory. The proof is the Quranic verse 2:196 about fidyah for shaving due to illness, extended to the forced case by analogy (dalalah). The passage further provides a hierarchy of choices: between carrion and game, the muhrim eats carrion to avoid two prohibitions; between game slaughtered by a muhrim and carrion, he eats game because its prohibition is incidental; between live game and another Muslim's property, he eats game because property rights of a human are given priority due to the owner's need. The final ruling: the forced muhrim must pay the penalty (jaza') if he kills and eats game, despite the permission, because the act is voluntary in response to the animal's attack, not a divine affliction.

Key Terms
المضطر (al-mudtarr): A muhrim forced by extreme hunger (makhmasah).
المؤذى (al-mu'dhi): A noxious animal that attacks, like a predator.
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The penalty or compensation due for killing game in ihram, often a sacrificial animal.
الدلالة (al-dalalah): Indication or inferred meaning; used to extend a ruling by analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim is forced by extreme hunger and kills a deer. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No penalty because of necessity
B) Penalty is wajib
C) He must fast three days
D) He must only repent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, killing game in ihram is penalty-free if the animal attacks, but incurs penalty when done out of hunger — the difference lies in the nature of permission from the Lawgiver.

Penalty for Killing a Pigeon with Feathered Legs (Hamam Masrul) and a Tame Gazelle: The Hanafi-Maliki Dispute
Dispute on penalty for hamam masrul and tame gazelle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument for imposing a penalty on killing a hamam masrul and a tame gazelle while in ihram, and evaluate the Maliki counterargument.

Core Idea
Game by original creation requires penalty; accidental domestication does not change its ruling.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses two specific cases where the muhrim must pay a penalty for killing animals that appear tame but are originally game. The Hanafi school argues that their fundamental nature as game determines the ruling, while the Maliki school disagrees regarding the pigeon. Understanding this dispute clarifies the principle that original creation (asl al-khilqa) takes precedence over accidental attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two animals and the Hanafi ruling
Note that both the hamam masrul (pigeon with feathered legs) and the tame gazelle incur a penalty (jaza') when killed by a muhrim, according to the Hanafi school. This is because they are considered game by original creation.
The term 'tame' (musta'nis) is a qualification specific to these two; for other clear game, penalty is agreed upon.
2
State the Maliki objection for the hamam masrul
Imam Malik holds that no penalty is required for the hamam masrul because it is tame and cannot fly well, comparing it to a duck, which is not game.
The Maliki view is based on observable behavior (inability to fly) rather than origin.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response: the pigeon is game by original creation (asl al-khilqa). Its slow rising due to weight does not remove it from being game. The condition of optional slaughter (dhabh ikhtiyari) does not indicate it is not game.
The proof relies on the animal's fundamental nature, not its accidental state.
4
Apply the same principle to the tame gazelle
Similarly, a tame gazelle retains its original ruling as game. Domestication is accidental and does not nullify the original ruling, just as a camel that goes wild is treated as game for slaughter purposes.
Analogy (qiyas) is used: the wild camel analogy shows that accidental change does not alter the original hukm.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, a Maliki muhrim would not pay a penalty for killing a hamam masrul, while a Hanafi muhrim would. Both schools agree on the penalty for the tame gazelle, but the Hanafi ruling applies even if the gazelle is tame.
The dispute is limited to the hamam masrul; the tame gazelle is agreed upon between the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمحرم ذبح شاة إلخ : أي يجوز للمحرم ذبح شاة وغيرها إلى البط؛ للإجماع، ولأنه ممنوع عن الصيد، وهذه الحيوانات ليست بصيود، ولو نزا ظبي على شاة، فالولد كأمه والحاصل أن الذبح مباح للمحرم، وتركه أولى فعله (فتح) أهلي يوجد في المساكن والحياض، أما الذي يطير فيجب الجزاء بقتله. (ط)] مطلقا مؤذيا أو لا، وإن حرم قتل غير المؤذي ككلب أهلي لا يؤذي. (ط) وعليه الجزاء إلخ: أي يجب الجزاء على المحرم بذبح حمام مسرول - بفتح الواو - هو الذي كثر ريشه على رجليه فصار بطيء النهوض، وإنما به؛ لأنه يشبه السراويل، وقال مالك: لا يجب فيه شيء؛ لأنه ألوف مستأنس، ولا يمتنع بجناحه فصار كالبط ولنا أنه صيد بأصل الخلقة، وإنما لا يطير لثقله وبطوء نهوضه، وذلك لا يخرجه عن أن يكون صيدا، واشتراط ذكاة الاختيار لا يدل على أنه ليس بصيد، وقوله: "ظبي مستأنس" أي عليه الجزاء بذبحه؛ لأن الاستيناس عارض فلا يبطل به الحكم الأصلي، كالبعير إذا ندّ يأخذ حكم الصيد في حل الذكاة لا غير حتى لا يحرم عقره على المحرم والظبي صيد في الأصل؛ ولهذا يجب الجزاء فيهما؛ لأنهما صيدان بأصل الخلقة. (فتح عيني) مستأنس: قيد بهما؛ لأن في غيرهما يجب الجزاء بالاتفاق. (ط)
And for the muhrim to slaughter a sheep etc.: i.e., it is permissible for the muhrim to slaughter a sheep and others up to duck; due to consensus, and because he is prohibited from game, and these animals are not game. And if a gazelle mates with a sheep, the offspring follows its mother. The summary is that slaughter is permissible for the muhrim, but leaving it is better than doing it. (Fath) Domestic: found in houses and ponds. As for that which flies, penalty is required for killing it. (T) Absolutely, whether harmful or not. And it is prohibited to kill non-harmful ones like a domestic dog that does not harm. (T) And upon him penalty, etc.: i.e., penalty is obligatory on the muhrim for slaughtering a hamam masrul – with fatha on the waw – it is the one with many feathers on its legs, becoming slow to get up, and it is so called because it resembles trousers (sarawil). Malik said: Nothing is required for it, because it is tame and domesticated, and does not resist with its wings, thus like a duck. Our proof: it is game by original creation; it only does not fly due to its weight and slow rising, and that does not remove it from being game. The condition of optional slaughter (dhabh ikhtiyari) does not indicate that it is not game. And his saying: 'tame gazelle', i.e., penalty is due upon slaughtering it, because domestication is accidental so it does not nullify the original ruling, like a camel that goes wild takes the ruling of game in permissibility of slaughter only, so that it is not prohibited to hamstring it for the muhrim. And the gazelle is originally game; therefore penalty is due on both of them, because they are game by original creation. (Fath, 'Ayni) 'Tame' is a qualification for both, because in other than them, penalty is due by agreement. (T)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on permissible slaughter for a muhrim. It begins by stating that the muhrim may slaughter domestic animals like sheep, cow, camel, chicken, and duck due to consensus, as these are not game. It notes that if a gazelle mates with a sheep, the offspring takes the mother's ruling. The preferred opinion is to leave slaughter if possible, though it is permissible. Then it clarifies that domestic animals are those found in houses; flying birds require penalty. The core of the passage addresses two specific types of game: the 'hamam masrul' (a pigeon with feathered legs) and the 'tame gazelle'. For the hamam, Imam Malik holds no penalty because it is tame and cannot fly well, comparing it to a duck. The Hanafi position argues that it is game by original creation; its inability to fly is due to weight, which does not change its nature. Likewise, for a tame gazelle, domestication is accidental and does not alter its original ruling as game. An analogy is given: a camel that goes wild is treated as game for slaughter purposes only. Thus, both require the full penalty (jaza') because they are fundamentally game. The word 'tame' is a qualification specific to these two; for other clear game, penalty is agreed upon.

Key Terms
حمام مسرول (hamam masrul): A pigeon with abundant feathers on its legs, making it slow to fly; resembles trousers.
ظبي مستأنس (dhabi musta'nis): A tame gazelle; domestication is accidental and does not change its original status as game.
جزاء (jaza'): Penalty or compensation required for killing game while in ihram, typically a sacrificial animal or its equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A muhrim must pay a penalty for killing a hamam masrul or a tame gazelle because they are game by original creation, and accidental domestication does not alter their fundamental legal status.

Muhrim Slaughtering Game: Carrion and Penalty
Ruling on a muhrim who slaughters game: meat is carrion, penalty for eating differs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game slaughtered by a muhrim, identify the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i position, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference over penalty.

Core Idea
Game slaughtered by a muhrim is carrion for all; if he eats it, Abu Hanifa requires compensation, the Sahibayn do not.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a layered fiqh discussion: the status of game killed by a muhrim, the inter-madhhab disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, and an intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on the penalty for eating it. Understanding these distinctions sharpens your grasp of how ihram prohibitions interact with underlying rulings on carrion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the base ruling
What is the act being ruled upon (a muhrim slaughtering game) and what is the Hanafi ruling on the meat? The meat is considered carrion (maytah) and prohibited for all.
This is the consensus among the three Hanafi imams.
2
Locate the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the meat is halal for others (non-slaughterers) because the slaughter is valid in reality. Identify the opposing school's position and its basis.
Shafi'i: dhabh exists, so it operates except for the slaughterer due to his prohibition.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school proves its position from the Quranic terminology: Allah calls killing game in ihram 'qatl' (killing), not dhabh. Why does this indicate the slaughter is invalid?
The term qatl implies destruction without ritual validity.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree on whether the muhrim slaughterer must pay the value if he eats the meat. Identify each position and the reasoning behind it.
Abu Hanifa: dual prohibition (carrion + ihram violation) requires compensation. Sahibayn: only carrion, thus only repentance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
What is the practical consequence of this difference? Which position is relied upon for fatwa? The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's: the slaughterer must pay the value.
Al-'Ayni and al-Fath prefer Abu Hanifa's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ذبح محرم صيداً: أي وكذا لو ذبحه الحلال في الحرم حرم أكله على الذابح وعلى غيره، وذبيحته ميتة كذبيحة المجوسي، وقال الشافعي لا يحل للمحرم القاتل ويحل لغيره؛ لأن الذكاة موجودة حقيقة فتعمل عملها على الذابح؛ لارتكابه النهي فيبقى في حق غيره من المحرمين أو في غيرهم، بل في حق نفسه بعد التحلل على الأصل، ولنا أنه تعالى سماه قتلاً، فدل على أنه ليس بذكاة. غير لا أنه حرم حرم أكله وذبيحته ميتة سواء أكله محرم أو لا . (ط) وغرم بأكله : [ أي لو أكل المحرم الذابح من لحم الذي ذبحه ضمن قيمة ما أكل. (ع)] هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : لا يضمن الذابح أيضاً بأكله؛ لأنه ميتة، وتناول الميتة يوجب إلا الاستغفار وله أنه حرام عليه ؛ لكونه محظور إحرامه لا لكونه ميتة بخلاف محرم آخر، فإن حرمة تناوله كونه ميتة لا إحرامه؛ ولهذا لا يجوز للحلال أكله والحاصل أن حرمته على الذابح من وجهين: كونه ميتة وتناوله محظور إحرامه فأضيفت حرمة التناول إلى إحرامه، فوجب عليه قيمة ما أكل، وأما المحرم الآخر، فالحرمة عليه من جهة واحدة وهي كونه ميتة فيه فلم يتناول محظور إحرامه، ولا شيء بأكل الميتة سوى التوبة والاستغفار. (عيني، فتح)
And if a muhrim slaughters game: i.e., and likewise if a non-muhrim (halal) slaughters it in the Haram, its meat is prohibited for the slaughterer and others, and his slaughtered animal is carrion like the slaughter of a Majusi. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible for the muhrim killer, but permissible for others, because the slaughter (dhabh) exists in reality, so it operates its effect; due to his committing the prohibition, it remains in effect regarding others among the muhrims or others, even regarding himself after tahlil according to the original. Our proof: Allah called it 'qatl' (killing), which indicates it is not dhabh (proper slaughter). 'Not' that it is prohibited, its consumption is prohibited and its slaughtered animal is carrion whether a muhrim eats it or not. (T) And he is penalized by eating it: i.e., if the muhrim slaughterer eats from the meat of what he slaughtered, he guarantees the value of what he ate. (Ayni) This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The slaughterer is not liable by eating it either, because it is carrion, and consuming carrion only requires seeking forgiveness. And for him (Abu Hanifa) it is prohibited for him because it is a prohibition of his ihram, not because it is carrion, unlike another muhrim, for the prohibition for him is that it is carrion, not his ihram. Hence, it is not permissible for a non-muhrim to eat it. The summary: its prohibition on the slaughterer is from two aspects: being carrion and consuming a prohibition of his ihram, so the prohibition of consumption is added to his ihram, thus he must guarantee the value of what he ate. As for another muhrim, the prohibition is from one aspect: being carrion, so he did not consume a prohibition of his ihram, and there is no liability for eating carrion except repentance and seeking forgiveness. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage examines the case where a muhrim slaughters game. The ruling is that such slaughter does not make the meat halal; it is considered carrion (maytah), like the slaughter of a Majusi. The meat is prohibited for the slaughterer and for anyone else, whether in ihram or not. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the actual slaughter (dhabh) is valid, so the meat is halal for others except the slaughterer; the Hanafi position rejects this because the Qur'an calls killing game while in ihram 'qatl' (killing), not dhabh, implying no valid slaughter. The text then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the muhrim slaughterer eats the meat, does he owe the value? According to Abu Hanifa, he must pay the value (gharam), because the meat is forbidden to him specifically due to his ihram, in addition to being carrion. This dual prohibition makes his consumption a violation of ihram, requiring compensation. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he is not liable, because the meat is carrion, and consumption of carrion only requires repentance. The difference hinges on whether the prohibition for the slaughterer is primarily due to ihram or due to being carrion. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is usually Abu Hanifa's, as the text's conclusion favors it. For another muhrim who eats it, only repentance is required, as the prohibition is solely from being carrion.

Key Terms
الذكاة (al-dhakāt): Ritual slaughter that renders meat halal; absent in muhrim's slaughter.
الجزاء (al-jazā'): The penalty (usually a sacrifice or compensation) for violating ihram through killing game.
محرم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah, subject to specific prohibitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a muhrim slaughters game and eats it, what must he do?
A) Only repent
B) Pay the value of what he ate
C) Sacrifice a sheep
D) Fast three days

Key Takeaway
Game slaughtered by a muhrim is carrion for all; the slaughterer who eats it must pay its value according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Halal Hunting Game for Muhrim: Conditions and Ruling
Ruling on meat of game hunted by a halal person for a muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game hunted by a halal person for a muhrim and explain the conditions under which it is permissible, including the inter-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
Muhrim may eat game hunted by a halal person, unless he indicated or commanded the hunt.

When a muhrim is on pilgrimage, dietary restrictions on hunting apply strictly. However, what about game hunted by a non-muhrim (halal) person? This passage clarifies the Hanafi position that the meat is permissible, provided the muhrim did not direct the hunt. It also addresses the opposing views of Malik and Shafi'i, who hold stricter prohibitions. Understanding these differences is crucial for a muhrim's valid practice of Hajj and Umrah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact Hanafi ruling: the meat is halal for the muhrim if two conditions are met — the game is from the land of tahlil (outside the Haram) and the muhrim did not indicate (dalala) or order (amr) the hunt.
Note the term 'halal' here means non-muhrim.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State that Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that if the game is hunted specifically for the muhrim, it becomes haram, even without indication.
Their proof is a hadith: 'Game is permissible for you as long as you do not hunt it or it is hunted for you.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith cited by the opponent. It appears to prohibit game hunted 'for you'. Understand how they interpret this as a general prohibition.
Abu Dawud narrates it.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by (1) declaring the hadith weak, or (2) interpreting it as referring to hunting upon direct command of the muhrim. They present the counter-proof of Abu Qatada's wild donkey hunt, where the Prophet ﷺ permitted the muhrim companions to eat even though it was hunted for them.
This shows that mere intention for the muhrim does not prohibit.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis allow a muhrim to eat game hunted for him without direction; Malikis/Shafi'is do not. This affects what a muhrim can accept as hospitality during Hajj.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the permissive position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحل له أي حل للمحرم لحم ما صاده حلال من أرض الحل وذبحه في الحل؛ لأن ما ذبحه الحلال في الحرم محرم وميتة، سواء كان صاد لأجله أو لا، بشرط أن لا يكون دلالة المحرم عليه ولا أمره بصيده، وقال مالك: إن اصطاده الحلال لأجل المحرم لا يحل لأن يتناوله وبه قال الشافعي؛ لقوله عليه : "الصيد حلال لكم ما لم تصيدوه أو يصاد لكم"، رواه أبو داود ولنا أن أبا قتادة الله لم يصد حمار الوحش لنفسه خاصة، بل له ولأصحابه، وهم محرمون فأباحه لهم عليها و لم يحرمه بإرادته أن يكون لهم، وما روياه ضعيف ولئن صح يحمل على ما إذا صيد له بأمره، وإنما قيد الحل بعدم الدلالة والأمر؛ لأنه لو دل أو أمر لا يحل وعليه الجزاء وكما يجب الجزاء بالدلالة فكذا بالإشارة بشرط أن لا يكون للقاتل علم بالصيد قبل الدلالة أو الإشارة (عيني، فتح) وذبحه: سواء اصطاده لأجله أولا . (ط)
And it is permissible for him: i.e., permissible for the muhrim the meat of what a non-muhrim (halal) hunted from the land of tahlil (outside Haram) and slaughtered in the tahlil, because what a non-muhrim slaughtered in the Haram is prohibited and carrion, whether he hunted it for him or not, on condition that the muhrim did not indicate (dalaalatan) the game to him nor order him to hunt it. Malik said: If the non-muhrim hunted it for the sake of the muhrim, it is not permissible for him to eat it, and al-Shafi'i agreed, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Game is permissible for you as long as you do not hunt it or it is hunted for you.' Narrated by Abu Dawud. Our proof: Abu Qatada (may Allah be pleased with him) did not hunt the wild donkey specifically for himself, but for himself and his companions, and they were muhrim, and he (the Prophet) permitted it for them and did not prohibit it because of his intention that it be for them. And what they narrated is weak, and if it is authentic, it is interpreted to mean when it is hunted upon his command. And the permission is restricted to non-indication and non-command; because if he indicated or commanded, it is not permissible and penalty is due. And just as penalty is required by indication, so also by a gesture, on condition that the killer did not have knowledge of the game before the indication or gesture. ('Ayni, Fath) And his slaughter: whether he hunted it for him or not. (T)
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling when a non-muhrim (halal) person hunts game and the meat is intended for a muhrim. The Hanafi position: the meat is permissible for the muhrim, provided the muhrim did not indicate or command the hunting. The proof is the hadith of Abu Qatada, who hunted a wild donkey while the Prophet's companions were in ihram, and the Prophet permitted them to eat it. This shows that a halal person may hunt for a muhrim without penalty. Imam Malik and al-Shafi'i oppose this, citing a hadith: 'Game is permissible for you as long as you do not hunt it or it is hunted for you.' The Hanafi response: that hadith is weak, or if authentic, it refers to hunting upon the muhrim's direct command. Thus, as long as the muhrim did not guide or request, the meat is halal. If he does indicate or command, the meat is haram for him and he must pay the penalty (jaza'). This includes gestures, provided the hunter had no prior knowledge of the game. The condition is that the shooting occurs in the land of tahlil (outside the Haram); if in Haram, it is carrion regardless.

Key Terms
الدلالة (al-dalālah): Indication by the muhrim of the game's location to the hunter, which renders the meat haram.
الإشارة (al-ishārah): A gesture by the muhrim pointing out the game, treated like explicit command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The muhrim indicating or commanding the hunt
C) The game being small (e.g., rabbit versus deer)
D) The hunter being a close relative of the muhrim

Key Takeaway
A muhrim may eat game hunted by a non-muhrim, provided he did not direct the hunt in any way.

Penalty for Halal Slaughter of Game in Haram
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi penalty for a halal person killing game in the Haram, explain the Hanafi proof, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Security due to Haram is considered only once; for a halal person, it is value, not kaffarah.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a person not in ihram (halal) kills game within the sacred precincts of the Haram. What penalty applies? The Hanafi school distinguishes this from the case of a muhrim, relying on a hadith and the principle that security cannot be considered twice. Let's unpack the reasoning and the inter-madhhab disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a halal (non-muhrim) person slaughtering game within the Haram. The Hanafi ruling is wajib: pay the value of the game as charity to the poor.
Note the qualification 'halal' — this is essential for the distinction.
2
Trace the Hanafi dalil
The proof is the hadith 'La yunfaru sayduha' (its game is not to be chased away). By indication (dalala), this implies prohibition of killing, thus compensation (value) is due. Consensus (ijma') supports this.
The hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut, but the ruling is strengthened by ijma'.
3
Understand the distinction from a muhrim's case
If a muhrim kills game in the Haram, only one kaffarah applies for violating ihram, not an additional penalty for the Haram. The security (amn) can only be considered once for guarantee.
Ihram takes precedence because it carries the meaning of penalty (jaza').
4
Analyze the Shafi'i disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes the halal person to the muhrim, requiring kaffarah (e.g., sacrificing a sheep). The Hanafi response: the two cases differ because the muhrim's ihram already accounts for the security of the Haram.
This is a thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi says value, Shafi'i says kaffarah.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There are two narrations among Hanafis on whether a sacrificial animal (hady) is permitted instead of value. The mufta bihi is that only value is required.
The relied-upon position is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بذبح الحلال أي يجب بذبح الحلال صيد الحرم قيمة ذلك الصيد؛ لأن الصيد استحق الأمن بسبب الحرم؛ لقوله عليه: "لا ينفر صيدها ، فاستفيد منه بطريق الدلالة حرمة القتل، فتجب القيمة وعليه انعقد الإجماع. وقوله: " يتصدق بها أي على الفقراء سوى الأصول والفروع، ولا يشترط الإسلام ويجزئه الإطعام في صيد الحرم، وقيد بالحلال؛ لأن المحرم إذا قتل صيد الحرم يلزمه كفارة واحدة لأجل الإحرام لا لأجل الحرم؛ لأن معنى تفويت الأمن إذا اعتبر مرة لإيجاب الضمان لا يمكن اعتباره ثانياً بسبب الحرم، واعتبار الإحرام أولى؛ لما فيه معنى الجزاء، وقال الشافعي: يكفر قياساً على قتل المحرم، وفي جواز الهدي روايتان عن أصحابنا. (فتح، مستخلص، عيني)
And by the slaughter of a non-muhrim (halal): i.e., by the slaughter of a halal person of game in the Haram, the value of that game is obligatory, because the game earned security due to the Haram; due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Its game is not to be chased away,' so from it by way of indication (dalala) the prohibition of killing is understood, so value is obligatory, and upon that consensus has been formed. And his statement: 'He gives charity with it' i.e., to the poor, excluding ascendants and descendants. Islam is not a condition, and feeding suffices in the case of game in the Haram. It is qualified by 'halal' because if a muhrim kills game in the Haram, one kaffarah is necessary on him due to ihram, not due to the Haram, because the meaning of 'losing security' if considered once for the obligation of guarantee, it cannot be considered a second time due to the Haram, and considering ihram is more appropriate since it involves the meaning of penalty (jaza'). Al-Shafi'i said: He expiates (yukaffir) by analogy to the killing of a muhrim. And regarding the permissibility of a sacrificial animal (hady), there are two narrations from our companions. (Fath, Mustakhlas, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This final passage addresses the situation where a non-muhrim (halal person) slaughters game within the boundaries of the Haram (sanctuary of Makkah). The ruling: he must pay the value (qimah) of the game as charity to the poor, excluding his own ascendants and descendants. The proof is the hadith 'Its game is not to be chased away' (la yunfaru sayduha), from which the prohibition of killing is derived by indication (dalala). Consensus (ijma') supports this penalty. The qualification of 'halal' is important: if a muhrim kills game in the Haram, he incurs only one kaffarah for the violation of ihram, not an additional penalty for the Haram, because the security (amn) is considered only once; the ihram aspect takes precedence as it carries the concept of jaza' (sacrificial penalty). Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes the halal person's action to the muhrim's, requiring kaffarah. Among Hanafis, there are two narrations on whether a sacrificial animal (hady) is permitted instead of value. The relied-upon position is that only value is required. The charity is to be given to poor Muslims, and feeding them is also acceptable.

Key Terms
القيمة (al-qīmah): The monetary value of the game, to be given as charity when a halal person kills game in the Haram.
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): An expiatory sacrifice or penalty; for a halal person in Haram, Hanafi says value, not kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Pay the market value of the game as charity
C) Fast three days
D) No penalty if the game is not eaten

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a halal person killing game in the Haram must pay its value as charity, not a kaffarah, because the security of the Haram is considered only once.

Invalidity of a Muhrim's Slaughter of Game
Core ruling with evidence on invalidity of muhrim's slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a muhrim's slaughter of game is invalid and identify that the meat becomes carrion and is haram to eat.

Core Idea
A muhrim's slaughter of game is not valid tadhkiyah; the meat becomes carrion and is haram.

When a person in ihram (muhrim) slaughters game, the act does not constitute valid Islamic slaughter. Instead, the animal becomes carrion, and its meat is prohibited for everyone, regardless of ihram status. This lesson establishes the core ruling and its immediate consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a muhrim slaughtering game. The ruling is that the slaughter is invalid and the meat is haram.
The term 'game' refers to permissible land animals that are normally hunted.
2
Recognize the legal effect
The slaughtered animal is considered carrion (maytah), like the slaughter of a Magian.
Carrion is inherently impure and impermissible to consume.
3
Understand the scope of prohibition
The prohibition applies to the slaughterer and to all others, whether muhrim or not.
This is a comprehensive prohibition; no one may eat the meat.
4
Note the evidence
The Hanafi proof is that Allah called the act 'qatl' (killing) in the Quran, not tadhkiyah (slaughter).
Quran 5:95 uses the term 'qatl' for hunting game in ihram, indicating it is not valid slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ذبح محرم صيدا حرُمَ، وغرمَ بأكله، لا محرم آخر. ولو ذبح محرم صيداً: أي وكذا لو ذبحه الحلال في الحرم حرم أكله على الذابح وعلى غيره، وذبيحته ميتة كذبيحة المجوسي، وقال الشافعي لا يحل للمحرم القاتل ويحل لغيره؛ لأن الذكاة موجودة حقيقة فتعمل عملها على الذابح؛ لارتكابه النهي فيبقى في حق غيره من المحرمين أو في غيرهم، بل في حق نفسه بعد التحلل على الأصل، ولنا أنه تعالى سماه قتلاً، فدل على أنه ليس بذكاة. غير أنه حرم أكله وذبيحته ميتة سواء أكله محرم أو لا . (ط) وغرم بأكله : [ أي لو أكل المحرم الذابح من لحم الذي ذبحه ضمن قيمة ما أكل. (ع)] هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : لا يضمن الذابح أيضاً بأكله؛ لأنه ميتة، وتناول الميتة يوجب إلا الاستغفار وله أنه حرام عليه ؛ لكونه محظور إحرامه لا لكونه ميتة بخلاف محرم آخر، فإن حرمة تناوله كونه ميتة لا إحرامه؛ ولهذا لا يجوز للحلال أكله والحاصل أن حرمته على الذابح من وجهين: كونه ميتة وتناوله محظور إحرامه فأضيفت حرمة التناول إلى إحرامه، فوجب عليه قيمة ما أكل، وأما المحرم الآخر، فالحرمة عليه من جهة واحدة وهي كونه ميتة فيه فلم يتناول محظور إحرامه، ولا شيء بأكل الميتة سوى التوبة والاستغفار. (عيني، فتح)
If a muhrim slaughters game, it is prohibited, and he is liable for what he eats, not another muhrim. And if a muhrim slaughters game: meaning, similarly if a non-muhrim slaughters it in the Haram, its consumption is prohibited for the slaughterer and others, and his slaughtered animal is carrion like the slaughter of a Magian. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible for the muhrim who killed it but permissible for others, because effective slaughter exists in reality so it operates on the slaughterer due to his commission of the prohibition, so it remains in effect for others among the muhrim or others, even for himself after leaving ihram according to the principle. And for us (Hanafis): Allah Most High called it killing, indicating that it is not valid slaughter. However, its consumption is prohibited and its slaughtered animal is carrion whether a muhrim eats it or not. (T) And he is liable for his eating: meaning if the muhrim slaughterer eats from the meat he slaughtered, he must pay the value of what he ate. This is according to Abu Hanifah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: The slaughterer is not liable for his eating either, because it is carrion, and consuming carrion only requires seeking forgiveness. And for him (Abu Hanifah): It is prohibited for him because it is a restriction of his ihram, not because it is carrion, unlike another muhrim, for whom the prohibition of consumption is because it is carrion, not his ihram. Hence, a non-muhrim is not permitted to eat it. The conclusion: Its prohibition on the slaughterer is from two aspects: being carrion and its consumption being a prohibited act of his ihram, so the prohibition of consumption is attributed to his ihram, thus he must pay the value of what he ate. As for another muhrim, the prohibition upon him is from one aspect alone—it being carrion—so he has not consumed a prohibited act of his ihram, and there is nothing for eating carrion except repentance and seeking forgiveness. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that a muhrim's slaughter of game is invalid and the meat is carrion. It includes a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who holds the slaughter valid for others. The Hanafi proof is that Allah calls it 'qatl' (killing), not tadhkiyah. An intra-Hanafi disagreement is discussed: Abu Hanifah requires the muhrim to pay for any meat he eats, while the Sahibayn do not. The commentary explains the reasoning—the prohibition for the slaughterer has two aspects (carrion and ihram violation), so liability arises; for another muhrim, only the carrion aspect applies, so no liability.

Key Terms
محرم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah, prohibited from hunting.
ذبيحة ميتة (dhabihah maytah): An animal slaughtered in a manner that makes it carrion (invalid Islamically).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A muhrim's slaughter of game is invalid; the meat is carrion and haram for everyone.
